       Balaguer remained in power as president for 12 years. His tenure was a period of repression of human rights and civil liberties, ostensibly to keep pro-Castro or pro-communist parties out of power; 11,000 persons were killed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His rule was criticized for a growing disparity between rich and poor. It was, however, praised for an ambitious infrastructure program, which included construction of large housing projects, sports complexes, theaters, museums, aqueducts, roads, highways, and the massive
       <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Lighthouse" title="Columbus Lighthouse">
        Columbus Lighthouse
       </a>
       , completed in 1992 during a later tenure.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1978, Balaguer was succeeded in the presidency by opposition candidate
       <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Guzm%C3%A1n_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="Antonio Guzmán Fernández">
        Antonio Guzmán Fernández
       </a>
       , of the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Revolutionary_Party" title="Dominican Revolutionary Party">
        Dominican Revolutionary Party
       </a>
       (PRD). Another PRD win in 1982 followed, under
       <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Jorge_Blanco" title="Salvador Jorge Blanco">
        Salvador Jorge Blanco
       </a>
       . Under the PRD presidents, the Dominican Republic enjoyed a period of relative freedom and basic human rights.
      </p>
      <p>
       Balaguer regained the presidency in 1986 and was re-elected in 1990 and 1994, this last time just defeating PRD candidate
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Francisco_Pe%C3%B1a_G%C3%B3mez" title="José Francisco Peña Gómez">
        José Francisco Peña Gómez
       </a>
       , a former mayor of Santo Domingo. The 1994 elections were flawed, bringing on international pressure, to which Balaguer responded by scheduling another presidential contest in 1996.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIADemo_6-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIADemo-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       That year
       <a href="/wiki/Leonel_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="Leonel Fernández">
        Leonel Fernández
       </a>
       achieved the first-ever win for the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Liberation_Party" title="Dominican Liberation Party">
        Dominican Liberation Party
       </a>
       (PLD), which Bosch had founded in 1973 after leaving the PRD (which he also had founded). Fernández oversaw a fast-growing economy: growth averaged 7.7% per year, unemployment fell, and there were stable exchange and inflation rates.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coha_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-coha-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">
        21st century
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DM16Ago.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="898" data-file-width="1347" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/DM16Ago.png/220px-DM16Ago.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/DM16Ago.png/330px-DM16Ago.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/DM16Ago.png/440px-DM16Ago.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DM16Ago.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         President
         <a href="/wiki/Danilo_Medina" title="Danilo Medina">
          Danilo Medina
         </a>
         at the swearing in of new government cabinet
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 2000 the PRD's
       <a href="/wiki/Hip%C3%B3lito_Mej%C3%ADa" title="Hipólito Mejía">
        Hipólito Mejía
       </a>
       won the election. This was a time of economic troubles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coha_84-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-coha-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mejía was defeated in his re-election effort in 2004 by Leonel Fernández of the PLD. In 2008, Fernández was as elected for a third term.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fernández and the PLD are credited with initiatives that have moved the country forward technologically, such as the construction of the
       <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo_Metro" title="Santo Domingo Metro">
        Metro Railway
       </a>
       ("El Metro"). On the other hand, his administrations have been accused of corruption.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coha_84-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-coha-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Danilo_Medina" title="Danilo Medina">
        Danilo Medina
       </a>
       , of the PLD, was elected president in 2012 and re-elected in 2016. He campaigned on a platform of investing more in social programs and education and less in infrastructure.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Geography of the Dominican Republic">
        Geography of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="1338" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg/300px-Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg/450px-Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg/600px-Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_Republic_relief_location_map.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Dominican Republic's topography
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic is situated on the eastern part of the second largest island in the
       <a href="/wiki/Greater_Antilles" title="Greater Antilles">
        Greater Antilles
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">
        Hispaniola
       </a>
       . It shares the island roughly at a 2:1 ratio with
       <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">
        Haiti
       </a>
       . The country's area is reported variously as 48,442 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (18,704 sq mi) (by the embassy in the United States)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embassy_3-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-embassy-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 48,730 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (18,815 sq mi),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIADemo_6-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIADemo-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       making it the second largest country in the
       <a href="/wiki/Antilles" title="Antilles">
        Antilles
       </a>
       , after
       <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
        Cuba
       </a>
       . The Dominican Republic's capital and largest metropolitan area
       <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">
        Santo Domingo
       </a>
       is on the southern coast.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are many small offshore islands and
       <a href="/wiki/Cay" title="Cay">
        cays
       </a>
       that are part of the Dominican territory. The two largest islands near shore are
       <a href="/wiki/Saona_Island" title="Saona Island">
        Saona
       </a>
       , in the southeast, and Beata, in the southwest. To the north, at distances of 100–200 kilometres (62–124 mi), are three extensive, largely submerged
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bank_(topography)" title="Bank (topography)">
        banks
       </a>
       , which geographically are a southeast continuation of
       <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">
        the Bahamas
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Navidad_Bank" title="Navidad Bank">
        Navidad Bank
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Silver_Bank" title="Silver Bank">
        Silver Bank
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Mouchoir_Bank" title="Mouchoir Bank">
        Mouchoir Bank
       </a>
       . Navidad Bank and Silver Bank have been officially claimed by the Dominican Republic.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has four important mountain ranges. The most northerly is the
       <i>
        Cordillera Septentrional
       </i>
       ("Northern Mountain Range"), which extends from the northwestern coastal town of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Fernando_de_Monte_Cristi" title="San Fernando de Monte Cristi">
        Monte Cristi
       </a>
       , near the Haitian border, to the Samaná Peninsula in the east, running parallel to the Atlantic coast. The highest range in the Dominican Republic – indeed, in the whole of the West Indies – is the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cordillera_Central,_Dominican_Republic" title="Cordillera Central, Dominican Republic">
         Cordillera Central
        </a>
       </i>
       ("Central Mountain Range"). It gradually bends southwards and finishes near the town of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azua_de_Compostela" title="Azua de Compostela">
        Azua
       </a>
       , on the Caribbean coast.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constanza_(15196384558).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Constanza_%2815196384558%29.jpg/300px-Constanza_%2815196384558%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Constanza_%2815196384558%29.jpg/450px-Constanza_%2815196384558%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Constanza_%2815196384558%29.jpg/600px-Constanza_%2815196384558%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constanza_(15196384558).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Constanza,_Dominican_Republic" title="Constanza, Dominican Republic">
          Constanza
         </a>
         valley
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the Cordillera Central are the four highest peaks in the Caribbean:
       <a href="/wiki/Pico_Duarte" title="Pico Duarte">
        Pico Duarte
       </a>
       (3,098 metres or 10,164 feet above sea level), La Pelona (3,094 metres or 10,151 feet), La Rucilla (3,049 metres or 10,003 feet), and Pico Yaque (2,760 metres or 9,055 feet). In the southwest corner of the country, south of the Cordillera Central, there are two other ranges. The more northerly of the two is the
       <i>
        Sierra de Neiba
       </i>
       , while in the south the
       <i>
        Sierra de Bahoruco
       </i>
       is a continuation of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massif_de_la_Selle" title="Massif de la Selle">
        Massif de la Selle
       </a>
       in Haiti. There are other, minor mountain ranges, such as the
       <i>
        Cordillera Oriental
       </i>
       ("Eastern Mountain Range"),
       <i>
        Sierra Martín García
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Sierra de Yamasá
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        Sierra de Samaná
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Between the Central and Northern mountain ranges lies the rich and fertile
       <a href="/wiki/Cibao" title="Cibao">
        Cibao
       </a>
       valley. This major valley is home to the cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_los_Caballeros" title="Santiago de los Caballeros">
        Santiago
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concepci%C3%B3n_de_La_Vega" title="Concepción de La Vega">
        La Vega
       </a>
       and most of the farming areas in the nation. Rather less productive are the semi-arid San Juan Valley, south of the Central Cordillera, and the Neiba Valley, tucked between the Sierra de Neiba and the Sierra de Bahoruco. Much of the land in the
       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Enriquillo" title="Lake Enriquillo">
        Enriquillo
       </a>
       Basin is below sea level, with a hot, arid, desert-like environment. There are other smaller valleys in the mountains, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Constanza,_Dominican_Republic" title="Constanza, Dominican Republic">
        Constanza
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jarabacoa" title="Jarabacoa">
        Jarabacoa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Villa_Altagracia" title="Villa Altagracia">
        Villa Altagracia
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Bonao" title="Bonao">
        Bonao
       </a>
       valleys.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Llano Costero del Caribe
       </i>
       ("Caribbean Coastal Plain") is the largest of the plains in the Dominican Republic. Stretching north and east of Santo Domingo, it contains many sugar plantations in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">
        savannahs
       </a>
       that are common there. West of Santo Domingo its width is reduced to 10 kilometres (6.2 mi) as it hugs the coast, finishing at the mouth of the Ocoa River. Another large plain is the
       <i>
        Plena de Azua
       </i>
       ("Azua Plain"), a very arid region in
       <a href="/wiki/Azua_Province" title="Azua Province">
        Azua Province
       </a>
       . A few other small coastal plains are in the northern coast and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Pedernales_Province" title="Pedernales Province">
        Pedernales
       </a>
       Peninsula.
      </p>
      <p>
       Four major rivers drain the numerous mountains of the Dominican Republic. The
       <a href="/wiki/Yaque_del_Norte_River" title="Yaque del Norte River">
        Yaque del Norte
       </a>
       is the longest and most important Dominican river. It carries excess water down from the Cibao Valley and empties into Monte Cristi Bay, in the northwest. Likewise, the
       <a href="/wiki/Yuna_River" title="Yuna River">
        Yuna River
       </a>
       serves the Vega Real and empties into Samaná Bay, in the northeast. Drainage of the San Juan Valley is provided by the San Juan River,
       <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">
        tributary
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaque_del_Sur" title="Yaque del Sur">
        Yaque del Sur
       </a>
       , which empties into the Caribbean, in the south. The
       <a href="/wiki/Artibonite_River" title="Artibonite River">
        Artibonito
       </a>
       is the longest river of Hispaniola and flows westward into Haiti.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1920" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg/250px-Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg/375px-Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg/500px-Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_republic_Los_Haitises_mangroves.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Mangroves in Los Haitises National Park
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are many lakes and coastal lagoons. The largest lake is
       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Enriquillo" title="Lake Enriquillo">
        Enriquillo
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">
        salt lake
       </a>
       at 45 metres (148 ft) below sea level, the lowest point in the Caribbean. Other important lakes are Laguna de Rincón or Cabral, with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">
        fresh water
       </a>
       , and Laguna de Oviedo, a lagoon with
       <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">
        brackish water
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Dominican Republic is located near fault action in the Caribbean. In 1946 it suffered
       <a href="/wiki/1946_Dominican_Republic_earthquake" title="1946 Dominican Republic earthquake">
        a magnitude 8.1 earthquake
       </a>
       off the northeast coast. This triggered a
       <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">
        tsunami
       </a>
       that killed about 1,800, mostly in coastal communities. The wave was also recorded at Daytona Beach, Florida, and Atlantic City, New Jersey. The area remains at risk. Caribbean countries and the United States have collaborated to create tsunami warning systems and are mapping risk in low-lying areas.
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
        Climate
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Climate of the Dominican Republic">
        Climate of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="783" data-file-width="765" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg/300px-Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg/450px-Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg/600px-Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_Republic_koppen.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification_system" title="Köppen climate classification system">
          Köppen climate types
         </a>
         of the Dominican Republic
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has a
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest_climate" title="Tropical rainforest climate">
        tropical rainforest climate
       </a>
       in the coastal and lowland areas. Due to its diverse topography, Dominican Republic's climate shows considerable variation over short distances and is the most varied of all the Antilles. The annual average temperature is 25 °C (77 °F). At higher elevations the temperature averages 18 °C (64.4 °F) while near sea level the average temperature is 28 °C (82.4 °F). Low temperatures of 0 °C (32 °F) are possible in the mountains while high temperatures of 40 °C (104 °F) are possible in protected valleys. January and February are the coolest months of the year while August is the hottest month. Snowfall can be seen in rare occasions on the summit of
       <a href="/wiki/Pico_Duarte" title="Pico Duarte">
        Pico Duarte
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DRcli_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DRcli-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">
        wet season
       </a>
       along the northern coast lasts from November through January. Elsewhere the wet season stretches from May through November, with May being the wettest month. Average annual rainfall is 1,500 millimetres (59.1 in) countrywide, with individual locations in the Valle de Neiba seeing averages as low as 350 millimetres (13.8 in) while the Cordillera Oriental averages 2,740 millimetres (107.9 in). The driest part of the country lies in the west.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DRcli_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-DRcli-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclones" title="Tropical cyclones">
        Tropical cyclones
       </a>
       strike the Dominican Republic every couple of years, with 65% of the impacts along the southern coast. Hurricanes are most likely between August and October.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DRcli_86-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-DRcli-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The last major hurricane that struck the country was
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Georges" title="Hurricane Georges">
        Hurricane Georges
       </a>
       in 1998.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HURDAT_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HURDAT-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">
        Government and politics
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2936" data-file-width="4566" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg/300px-Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg/450px-Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg/600px-Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santo_Domingo_National_Palace.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/National_Palace_(Dominican_Republic)" title="National Palace (Dominican Republic)">
          National Palace
         </a>
         in Santo Domingo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Politics of the Dominican Republic">
        Politics of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic is a
       <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">
        representative democracy
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">
        democratic republic
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embassy_3-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-embassy-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIADemo_6-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIADemo-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with three branches of power: executive,
       <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">
        legislative
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">
        judicial
       </a>
       . The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="President of the Dominican Republic">
        president of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
       heads the executive branch and executes laws passed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Congress of the Dominican Republic">
        congress
       </a>
       , appoints the cabinet, and is commander in chief of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Military of the Dominican Republic">
        armed forces
       </a>
       . The president and vice-president run for office on the same ticket and are elected by direct vote for 4-year terms. The national legislature is bicameral, composed of a
       <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Senate of the Dominican Republic">
        senate
       </a>
       , which has 32 members, and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Deputies_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Chamber of Deputies of the Dominican Republic">
        Chamber of Deputies
       </a>
       , with 178 members.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Judicial authority rests with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Dominican_Republic#Judicial_branch" title="Government of the Dominican Republic">
        Supreme Court of Justice
       </a>
       's 16 members. They are appointed by a council composed of the president, the leaders of both houses of congress, the President of the Supreme Court, and an opposition or non–governing-party member. The court "alone hears actions against the president, designated members of his Cabinet, and members of Congress when the legislature is in session."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has a
       <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">
        multi-party political system
       </a>
       . Elections are held every two years, alternating between the
       <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Elections in the Dominican Republic">
        presidential elections
       </a>
       , which are held in years evenly divisible by four, and the congressional and municipal elections, which are held in even-numbered years not divisible by four. "International observers have found that presidential and congressional elections since 1996 have been generally free and fair."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Central Elections Board (JCE) of nine members supervises elections, and its decisions are unappealable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Starting from 2016, elections will be held jointly, after a constitutional reform.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Political_culture">
        Political culture
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The three major parties are the conservative
       <a href="/wiki/Social_Christian_Reformist_Party" title="Social Christian Reformist Party">
        Social Christian Reformist Party
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es">
         Partido Reformista Social Cristiano (PRSC)
        </span>
       </i>
       ), in power 1966–78 and 1986–96; the
       <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">
        social democratic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Revolutionary_Party" title="Dominican Revolutionary Party">
        Dominican Revolutionary Party
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es">
         Partido Revolucionario Dominicano (PRD)
        </span>
       </i>
       ), in power in 1963, 1978–86, and 2000–04; and the
       <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">
        centrist
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">
        liberal
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformist" title="Reformist">
        reformist
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Liberation_Party" title="Dominican Liberation Party">
        Dominican Liberation Party
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es">
         Partido de la Liberación Dominicana (PLD)
        </span>
       </i>
       ), in power 1996–2000 and since 2004.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic_presidential_election,_2008" title="Dominican Republic presidential election, 2008">
        presidential elections of 2008
       </a>
       were held on May 16, 2008, with incumbent Leonel Fernández winning 53% of the vote.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He defeated
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miguel_Vargas_Maldonado" title="Miguel Vargas Maldonado">
        Miguel Vargas Maldonado
       </a>
       , of the PRD, who achieved a 40.48% share of the vote.
       <a href="/wiki/Amable_Aristy" title="Amable Aristy">
        Amable Aristy
       </a>
       , of the PRSC, achieved 4.59% of the vote. Other minority candidates, which included former Attorney General
       <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Moreno" title="Guillermo Moreno">
        Guillermo Moreno
       </a>
       from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Movement_for_Independence,_Unity_and_Change" title="Movement for Independence, Unity and Change">
        Movement for Independence, Unity and Change
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es">
         Movimiento Independencia, Unidad y Cambio (MIUCA)
        </span>
       </i>
       ), and PRSC former presidential candidate and defector
       <a href="/wiki/Eduardo_Estrella" title="Eduardo Estrella">
        Eduardo Estrella
       </a>
       , obtained less than 1% of the vote.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic_presidential_election,_2012" title="Dominican Republic presidential election, 2012">
        2012 presidential elections
       </a>
       the incumbent president
       <a href="/wiki/Leonel_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="Leonel Fernández">
        Leonel Fernández
       </a>
       (PLD) declined his aspirations
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and instead the PLD elected
       <a href="/wiki/Danilo_Medina" title="Danilo Medina">
        Danilo Medina
       </a>
       as its candidate. This time the PRD presented ex-president
       <a href="/wiki/Hip%C3%B3lito_Mej%C3%ADa" title="Hipólito Mejía">
        Hipolito Mejia
       </a>
       as its choice. The contest was won by Medina with 51.21% of the vote, against 46.95% in favor of Mejia. Candidate Guillermo Moreno obtained 1.37% of the votes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2014 the
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Revolutionary_Party" title="Modern Revolutionary Party">
        Modern Revolutionary Party
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es">
         Partido revolucionario Moderno
        </span>
       </i>
       ) was created
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       by a faction of leaders from the PRD and has since become the predominant opposition party, polling in second place for the upcoming May 2016 general elections.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">
        Foreign relations
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Foreign relations of the Dominican Republic">
        Foreign relations of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has a close relationship with the United States and with the other states of the Inter-American system. The Dominican Republic has very strong ties and relations with Puerto Rico.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic's
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic%E2%80%93Haiti_relations" title="Dominican Republic–Haiti relations">
        relationship with neighbouring Haiti
       </a>
       is strained over mass Haitian migration to the Dominican Republic, with citizens of the Dominican Republic blaming the Haitians for increased crime and other social problems.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Dominican Republic is a regular member of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_Internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation Internationale de la Francophonie">
        Organisation Internationale de la Francophonie
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has a
       <a href="/wiki/Free-trade_area" title="Free-trade area">
        Free Trade Agreement
       </a>
       with the United States, Costa Rica, El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras and Nicaragua via the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic%E2%80%93Central_America_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="Dominican Republic–Central America Free Trade Agreement">
        Dominican Republic-Central America Free Trade Agreement
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       And an
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_Partnership_Agreements" title="Economic Partnership Agreements">
        Economic Partnership Agreement
       </a>
       with the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Community" title="Caribbean Community">
        Caribbean Community
       </a>
       via the
       <a href="/wiki/CARIFORUM" title="CARIFORUM">
        Caribbean Forum.
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Military">
        Military
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg/300px-Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg/450px-Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg/600px-Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_Commando_soldiers_of_the_MEFA_provide_security.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Dominican soldiers training in Santo Domingo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Military of the Dominican Republic">
        Military of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Congress of the Dominican Republic">
        Congress
       </a>
       authorizes a combined military force of 44,000 active duty personnel. Actual active duty strength is approximately 32,000. Approximately 50% of those are used for non-military activities such as security providers for government-owned non-military facilities, highway toll stations, prisons, forestry work, state enterprises, and private businesses. The commander in chief of the military is the president.
      </p>
      <p>
       The army is larger than the other services combined with approximately 20,000 active duty personnel, consisting of six infantry
       <a href="/wiki/Brigade" title="Brigade">
        brigades
       </a>
       , a combat support brigade, and a combat service support brigade. The air force operates two main bases, one in the southern region near Santo Domingo and one in the northern region near Puerto Plata. The navy operates two major naval bases, one in Santo Domingo and one in Las Calderas on the southwestern coast, and maintains 12 operational vessels. The Dominican Republic has the second largest military in the Caribbean region after Cuba.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The armed forces have organized a Specialized Airport Security Corps (CESA) and a Specialized Port Security Corps (CESEP) to meet international security needs in these areas. The secretary of the armed forces has also announced plans to form a specialized border corps (CESEF). The armed forces provide 75% of personnel to the National Investigations Directorate (DNI) and the Counter-Drug Directorate (DNCD).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Dominican National Police force contains 32,000 agents. The police are not part of the Dominican armed forces but share some overlapping security functions. Sixty-three percent of the force serve in areas outside traditional police functions, similar to the situation of their military counterparts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">
        Administrative divisions
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Provinces of the Dominican Republic">
        Provinces of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Municipalities of the Dominican Republic">
        Municipalities of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic is divided into 31
       <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Provinces of the Dominican Republic">
        provinces
       </a>
       . Santo Domingo, the capital, is designated
       <a href="/wiki/Distrito_Nacional" title="Distrito Nacional">
        Distrito Nacional
       </a>
       (National District). The provinces are divided into municipalities (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Municipio" title="Municipio">
         municipios
        </a>
       </i>
       ; singular
       <i>
        municipio
       </i>
       ). They are the second-level political and
       <a href="/wiki/Administrative_division" title="Administrative division">
        administrative subdivisions
       </a>
       of the country. The president appoints the governors of the 31 provinces. Mayors and municipal councils administer the 124 municipal districts and the National District (Santo Domingo). They are elected at the same time as congressional representatives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Economy of the Dominican Republic">
        Economy of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="862" data-file-width="1104" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg/400px-Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg/600px-Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg/800px-Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_exports_2009_Dominican_Republic.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A proportional representation of the Dominican Republic's exports
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic is the largest economy
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gdp_rank_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-gdp_rank-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (according to the U.S. State Department and the World Bank)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dominican_Republic_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dominican_Republic-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in the Caribbean and Central American region. It is an upper middle-income
       <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">
        developing country
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with a 2015 GDP per capita of $14,770, in
       <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">
        PPP
       </a>
       terms. Over the last two decades, the Dominican Republic have been standing out as one of the fastest-growing economies in the Americas – with an average real GDP growth rate of 5.4% between 1992 and 2014.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dominican_Republic_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dominican_Republic-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       GDP growth in 2014 and 2015 reached 7.3 and 7.0%, respectively, the highest in the Western Hemisphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_22-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the first half of 2016 the Dominican economy grew 7.4%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2015
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dominican_Republic&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , the average wage in nominal terms is 392 USD per month ($17,829 DOP).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Average2_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Average2-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the last three decades, the Dominican economy, formerly dependent on the export of agricultural commodities (mainly sugar, cocoa and coffee), has transitioned to a diversified mix of services, manufacturing, agriculture, mining, and trade. The service sector accounts for almost 60% of GDP; manufacturing, for 22%; tourism, telecommunications and finance are the main components of the service sector; however, none of them accounts for more than 10% of the whole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Dominican Republic has a stock market,
       <a href="/wiki/Bolsa_de_Valores_de_la_Rep%C3%BAblica_Dominicana" title="Bolsa de Valores de la República Dominicana">
        Bolsa de Valores de la Republica Dominicana (BVRD)
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and advanced telecommunication system and transportation infrastructure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-consulate_26-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-consulate-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIADemo_6-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIADemo-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       government corruption, and inconsistent electric service remain major problems. The country also has "marked income inequality."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIADemo_6-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIADemo-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       International migration affects the Dominican Republic greatly, as it receives and sends large flows of migrants. Mass illegal Haitian immigration and the integration of Dominicans of Haitian descent are major issues.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pinadep_102-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pinadep-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A large Dominican diaspora exists, mostly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominican_Americans_(Dominican_Republic)" title="Dominican Americans (Dominican Republic)">
        in the United States
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s0201_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-s0201-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       contributes to development, sending billions of dollars to Dominican families in remittances.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIADemo_6-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIADemo-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Remittance" title="Remittance">
        Remittances
       </a>
       in Dominican Republic increased to 4571.30 million USD in 2014 from 3333 million USD in 2013 (according to data reported by the Inter-American Development Bank). Economic growth takes place in spite of a chronic energy shortage,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which causes frequent blackouts and very high prices. Despite a widening merchandise
       <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_trade" title="Balance of trade">
        trade deficit
       </a>
       , tourism earnings and remittances have helped build
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_reserves" title="Foreign exchange reserves">
        foreign exchange reserves
       </a>
       . Following economic turmoil in the late 1980s and 1990, during which the gross domestic product (GDP) fell by up to 5% and consumer price inflation reached an unprecedented 100%, the Dominican Republic entered a period of growth and declining inflation until 2002, after which the economy entered a
       <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">
        recession
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This recession followed the collapse of the second-largest
       <a href="/wiki/Commercial_bank" title="Commercial bank">
        commercial bank
       </a>
       in the country,
       <a href="/wiki/Banco_Intercontinental" title="Banco Intercontinental">
        Baninter
       </a>
       , linked to a major incident of fraud valued at $3.5 billion. The Baninter fraud had a devastating effect on the Dominican economy, with GDP dropping by 1% in 2003 as inflation ballooned by over 27%. All defendants, including the star of the trial,
       <a href="/wiki/Ram%C3%B3n_B%C3%A1ez_Figueroa" title="Ramón Báez Figueroa">
        Ramón Báez Figueroa
       </a>
       (the great-grandson of President
       <a href="/wiki/Buenaventura_B%C3%A1ez" title="Buenaventura Báez">
        Buenaventura Báez
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_TonySmith_105-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYTimes_TonySmith-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       were convicted.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to the 2005 Annual Report of the United Nations Subcommittee on Human Development in the Dominican Republic, the country is ranked No. 71 in the world for resource availability, No. 79 for human development, and No. 14 in the world for resource mismanagement. These statistics emphasize national government corruption, foreign economic interference in the country, and the rift between the rich and poor.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has a noted problem of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor">
        child labor
       </a>
       in its coffee, rice, sugarcane, and tomato industries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The labor injustices in the sugarcane industry extend to forced labor according to the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Labor" title="United States Department of Labor">
        U.S. Department of Labor
       </a>
       . Three large groups own 75% of the land: the State Sugar Council (Consejo Estatal del Azúcar, CEA),
       <a href="/wiki/Vicini" title="Vicini">
        Grupo Vicini
       </a>
       , and Central Romana Corporation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Country_Studies-Library_Congress_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Country_Studies-Library_Congress-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb noresize tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:958px;">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="">
        <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santo_Domingo_Panorama.jpg" title="A panoramic view of the National District">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="1024" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Santo_Domingo_Panorama.jpg/950px-Santo_Domingo_Panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/Santo_Domingo_Panorama.jpg 1.5x" width="950"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Santo_Domingo_Panorama.jpg" title="File:Santo Domingo Panorama.jpg">
          </a>
         </div>
         A panoramic view of the National District
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Currency">
        Currency
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_peso" title="Dominican peso">
        Dominican peso
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican peso (DOP, or RD$)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XE_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-XE-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is the national currency, with the United States dollar (USD), the Euro (EUR), the Canadian dollar (CAD) and the Swiss franc (CHF) also accepted at most tourist sites. The exchange rate to the U.S. dollar, liberalized by 1985, stood at 2.70 pesos per dollar in August 1986,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pons_59-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-pons-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         p417, 428
        </span>
       </sup>
       14.00 pesos in 1993, and 16.00 pesos in 2000. Having jumped to 53.00 pesos per dollar in 2003, the rate was back down to around 31.00 pesos per dollar in 2004. As of November 2010 the rate was 37.00 pesos per dollar. In February 2015 the rate was 44.67 pesos per dollar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XE_108-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-XE-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of February 2017 the rate was 46.72 pesos per dollar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">
        Tourism
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Tourism in the Dominican Republic">
        Tourism in the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Creactive.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2968" data-file-width="3960" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Creactive.jpg/240px-Creactive.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Creactive.jpg/360px-Creactive.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Creactive.jpg/480px-Creactive.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Creactive.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Punta_Cana" title="Punta Cana">
          Punta Cana
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Tourism is one of the fueling factors in the Dominican Republic's economic growth. The Dominican Republic is the most popular tourist destination in the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">
        Caribbean
       </a>
       . With the construction of projects like
       <a href="/wiki/Cap_Cana" title="Cap Cana">
        Cap Cana
       </a>
       , San Souci Port in Santo Domingo,
       <a href="/wiki/Casa_de_Campo,_Dominican_Republic" title="Casa de Campo, Dominican Republic">
        Casa De Campo
       </a>
       and the Hard Rock Hotel &amp; Casino (ancient Moon Palace Resort) in
       <a href="/wiki/Punta_Cana" title="Punta Cana">
        Punta Cana
       </a>
       , the Dominican Republic expects increased tourism activity in the upcoming years.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">
        Ecotourism
       </a>
       has also been a topic increasingly important in this nation, with towns like
       <a href="/wiki/Jarabacoa" title="Jarabacoa">
        Jarabacoa
       </a>
       and neighboring
       <a href="/wiki/Constanza,_Dominican_Republic" title="Constanza, Dominican Republic">
        Constanza
       </a>
       , and locations like the
       <a href="/wiki/Pico_Duarte" title="Pico Duarte">
        Pico Duarte
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bahia_de_las_Aguilas" title="Bahia de las Aguilas">
        Bahia de las Aguilas
       </a>
       , and others becoming more significant in efforts to increase direct benefits from tourism. Most residents from other countries
       <a href="/wiki/Visa_policy_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Visa policy of the Dominican Republic">
        are required to get a tourist card
       </a>
       , depending on the country they live in.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">
        Infrastructure
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">
        Transportation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation_in_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Transportation in the Dominican Republic">
        Transportation in the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="800" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg/300px-Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg/450px-Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg/600px-Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Obelisco_Santo_Domingo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         El Malecon av. in
         <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">
          Santo Domingo
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The country has three national trunk highways, which connect every major town. These are
       <a href="/wiki/DR-1" title="DR-1">
        DR-1
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/DR-2" title="DR-2">
        DR-2
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/DR-3" title="DR-3">
        DR-3
       </a>
       , which depart from Santo Domingo toward the northern (Cibao), southwestern (Sur), and eastern (El Este) parts of the country respectively. These highways have been consistently improved with the expansion and reconstruction of many sections. Two other national highways serve as spur (
       <a href="/wiki/DR-5" title="DR-5">
        DR-5
       </a>
       ) or alternate routes (
       <a href="/wiki/DR-4" title="DR-4">
        DR-4
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to the national highways, the government has embarked on an expansive reconstruction of spur secondary routes, which connect smaller towns to the trunk routes. In the last few years the government constructed a 106-kilometer toll road that connects Santo Domingo with the country's northeastern peninsula. Travelers may now arrive in the Samaná Peninsula in less than two hours. Other additions are the reconstruction of the DR-28 (Jarabacoa – Constanza) and
       <a href="/wiki/DR-12" title="DR-12">
        DR-12
       </a>
       (Constanza – Bonao). Despite these efforts, many secondary routes still remain either unpaved or in need of maintenance. There is currently a nationwide program to pave these and other commonly used routes. Also, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santiago_light_rail" title="Santiago light rail">
        Santiago light rail
       </a>
       system is in planning stages but currently on hold.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Bus_service">
        Bus service
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are two main bus transportation services in the Dominican Republic: one controlled by the government, through the Oficina Técnica de Transito Terrestre (OTTT) and the Oficina Metropolitana de Servicios de Autobuses (OMSA), and the other controlled by private business, among them, Federación Nacional de Transporte La Nueva Opción (FENATRANO) and the Confederacion Nacional de Transporte (CONATRA). The government transportation system covers large routes in metropolitan areas such as Santo Domingo and Santiago.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are many privately owned bus companies, such as Metro Servicios Turísticos and Caribe Tours, that run daily routes.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Santo_Domingo_Metro">
        Santo Domingo Metro
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stodgo_metro.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="1024" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Stodgo_metro.jpg/300px-Stodgo_metro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Stodgo_metro.jpg/450px-Stodgo_metro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Stodgo_metro.jpg/600px-Stodgo_metro.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stodgo_metro.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A pair of 9000 series are tested on the Santo Domingo Metro.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo_Metro" title="Santo Domingo Metro">
        Santo Domingo Metro
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has a
       <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">
        rapid transit
       </a>
       system in
       <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">
        Santo Domingo
       </a>
       , the country's capital. It is the most extensive metro system in the insular
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">
        Caribbean
       </a>
       and Central American region by length and number of stations. The Santo Domingo Metro is part of a major "National Master Plan" to improve transportation in Santo Domingo as well as the rest of the nation. The first line was planned to relieve traffic congestion in the
       <a href="/wiki/DR-1" title="DR-1">
        Máximo Gómez
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/DR-13" title="DR-13">
        Hermanas Mirabal Avenue
       </a>
       . The second line, which opened in April 2013, is meant to relieve the congestion along the
       <a href="/wiki/DR-13" title="DR-13">
        Duarte-Kennedy-Centenario
       </a>
       Corridor in the city from west to east. The current length of the Metro, with the sections of the two lines open as of August 2013, is 27.35 kilometres (16.99 mi). Before the opening of the second line, 30,856,515 passengers rode the Santo Domingo Metro in 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SD-stats_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SD-stats-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With both lines opened, ridership increased to 61,270,054 passengers in 2014.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">
        Communications
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Telecommunications in the Dominican Republic">
        Telecommunications in the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has a well developed telecommunications infrastructure, with extensive mobile phone and
       <a href="/wiki/Landline" title="Landline">
        landline
       </a>
       services.
       <a href="/wiki/Cable_Internet_access" title="Cable Internet access">
        Cable Internet
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_subscriber_line" title="Digital subscriber line">
        DSL
       </a>
       are available in most parts of the country, and many
       <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">
        Internet service providers
       </a>
       offer
       <a href="/wiki/3G" title="3G">
        3G
       </a>
       wireless internet service. The Dominican Republic became the second country in Latin America to have 4G LTE wireless service. The reported speeds are from 256 kbit/s or 128 kbit/s for residential services, up to 5 Mbit/s or 1 Mbit/s for residential service.
      </p>
      <p>
       For commercial service there are speeds from 256 kbit/s up to 154 Mbit/s. (Each set of numbers denotes
       <a href="/wiki/Cable_Internet_access" title="Cable Internet access">
        downstream/upstream speed
       </a>
       ; that is, to the user/from the user.) Projects to extend
       <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">
        Wi-Fi
       </a>
       hot spots have been made in Santo Domingo. The country's commercial radio stations and television stations are in the process of transferring to the
       <a href="/wiki/Data_transmission" title="Data transmission">
        digital spectrum
       </a>
       , via
       <a href="/wiki/HD_Radio" title="HD Radio">
        HD Radio
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">
        HDTV
       </a>
       after officially adopting
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_Television_Systems_Committee_standards" title="Advanced Television Systems Committee standards">
        ATSC
       </a>
       as the digital medium in the country with a switch-off of analog transmission by September 2015. The telecommunications regulator in the country is INDOTEL (
       <i>
        Instituto Dominicano de Telecomunicaciones
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The largest telecommunications company is
       <a href="/wiki/Claro_Rep%C3%BAblica_Dominicana" title="Claro República Dominicana">
        Claro
       </a>
       – part of
       <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Slim" title="Carlos Slim">
        Carlos Slim
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Am%C3%A9rica_M%C3%B3vil" title="América Móvil">
        América Móvil
       </a>
       – which provides wireless, landline, broadband, and
       <a href="/wiki/IPTV" title="IPTV">
        IPTV
       </a>
       services. In June 2009 there were more than 8 million phone line subscribers (land and cell users) in the D.R., representing 81% of the country's population and a fivefold increase since the year 2000, when there were 1.6 million. The communications sector generates about 3.0% of the GDP.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LD2009-06-05_111-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LD2009-06-05-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There were 2,439,997 Internet users in March 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In November 2009, the Dominican Republic became the first Latin American country to pledge to include a "gender perspective" in every information and communications technology (ICT) initiative and policy developed by the government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is part of the regional
       <a href="/wiki/ELAC_Action_Plans" title="ELAC Action Plans">
        eLAC2010
       </a>
       plan. The tool the Dominicans have chosen to design and evaluate all the public policies is the APC
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_Evaluation_Methodology_(GEM)" title="Gender Evaluation Methodology (GEM)">
        Gender Evaluation Methodology (GEM)
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electricity">
        Electricity
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Electricity sector in the Dominican Republic">
        Electricity sector in the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Electric power service has been unreliable since the Trujillo era, and as much as 75% of the equipment is that old. The country's antiquated power grid causes transmission losses that account for a large share of billed electricity from generators. The privatization of the sector started under a previous administration of Leonel Fernández.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coha_84-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-coha-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The recent investment in a "Santo Domingo–Santiago Electrical Highway" to carry 345 kW power,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DT2009-04-29_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DT2009-04-29-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with reduced losses in transmission, is being heralded as a major capital improvement to the national grid since the mid-1960s.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the Trujillo regime electrical service was introduced to many cities. Almost 95% of usage was not billed at all. Around half of the Dominican Republic's 2.1 million houses have no meters and most do not pay or pay a fixed monthly rate for their electric service.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DT2006-06-01_115-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DT2006-06-01-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Household and general electrical service is delivered at 110
       <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">
        volts
       </a>
       alternating at 60
       <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">
        Hz
       </a>
       . Electrically powered items from the United States work with no modifications. The majority of the Dominican Republic has access to electricity. Tourist areas tend to have more reliable power, as do business, travel, healthcare, and vital infrastructure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concentrated efforts were announced to increase efficiency of delivery to places where the collection rate reached 70%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LD2007-04-11_117-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LD2007-04-11-117">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The electricity sector is highly politicized. Some generating companies are undercapitalized and at times unable to purchase adequate fuel supplies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussdnote_85-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-ussdnote-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">
        Water supply and sanitation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Water supply and sanitation in the Dominican Republic">
        Water supply and sanitation in the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic has achieved impressive increases in access to
       <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">
        water supply
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">
        sanitation
       </a>
       over the past two decades. However, the quality of water supply and sanitation services remains poor, despite the country's high economic growth during the 1990s. Although the coverage of
       <a href="/wiki/Improved_water_source" title="Improved water source">
        improved water sources
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Improved_sanitation" title="Improved sanitation">
        improved sanitation
       </a>
       is with 86% respectively 83% relatively high, there are substantial regional differences. Poor households exhibit lower levels of access: only 56% of poor households are connected to water house connections as opposed to 80% of non-poor households. Just 20% of poor households have access to sewers, as opposed to 50% for the non-poor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CEA_118-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CEA-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Society">
        Society
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Demographics of the Dominican Republic">
        Demographics of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_Rep_demography.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="725" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Dominican_Rep_demography.png/350px-Dominican_Rep_demography.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Dominican_Rep_demography.png/525px-Dominican_Rep_demography.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Dominican_Rep_demography.png/700px-Dominican_Rep_demography.png 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dominican_Rep_demography.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Dominican Republic's population (1961–2003)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Dominican Republic's population was 10,648,791 in 2016.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_119-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2010 31.2% of the population was under 15 years of age, with 6% of the population over 65 years of age.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPP_2012_120-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WPP_2012-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There were 103 males for every 100 females in 2007.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIADemo_6-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIADemo-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The annual population growth rate for 2006–2007 was 1.5%, with the projected population for the year 2015 being 10,121,000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-esa-121">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The population density in 2007 was 192 per km² (498 per sq mi), and 63% of the population lived in urban areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
        <a href="#cite_note-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The southern coastal plains and the Cibao Valley are the most densely populated areas of the country. The capital city Santo Domingo had a population of 2,907,100 in 2010.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationsencyclopedia.com_123-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nationsencyclopedia.com-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Other important cities are
       <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_los_Caballeros" title="Santiago de los Caballeros">
        Santiago de los Caballeros
       </a>
       (
       <abbr title="population">
        pop.
       </abbr>
       745,293),
       <a href="/wiki/La_Romana,_Dominican_Republic" title="La Romana, Dominican Republic">
        La Romana
       </a>
       (pop. 214,109),
       <a href="/wiki/San_Pedro_de_Macor%C3%ADs" title="San Pedro de Macorís">
        San Pedro de Macorís
       </a>
       (pop. 185,255), Higüey (153,174),
       <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_de_Macor%C3%ADs" title="San Francisco de Macorís">
        San Francisco de Macorís
       </a>
       (pop. 132,725),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Felipe_de_Puerto_Plata" title="San Felipe de Puerto Plata">
        Puerto Plata
       </a>
       (pop. 118,282), and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concepci%C3%B3n_de_la_Vega" title="Concepción de la Vega">
        La Vega
       </a>
       (pop. 104,536). Per the United Nations, the urban population growth rate for 2000–2005 was 2.3%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationsencyclopedia.com_123-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nationsencyclopedia.com-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">
        Ethnic groups
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/People_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="People of the Dominican Republic">
        People of the Dominican Republic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="681" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg/300px-Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg/450px-Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg/600px-Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dominican-people-cibao-1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
